<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deutsche_Nationalmannschaft.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="843" data-file-width="900" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Deutsche_Nationalmannschaft.JPG/260px-Deutsche_Nationalmannschaft.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Deutsche_Nationalmannschaft.JPG/390px-Deutsche_Nationalmannschaft.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Deutsche_Nationalmannschaft.JPG/520px-Deutsche_Nationalmannschaft.JPG 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deutsche_Nationalmannschaft.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
German national team in 2012</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>Germany women's national football team</b> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Deutsche Fußballnationalmannschaft der Frauen</i></span>) represents Germany in international <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_association_football" title="Women's association football">women's association football</a> and is governed by the <a href="/wiki/German_Football_Association" title="German Football Association">German Football Association</a> (DFB).</p>
<p>The German national team is one of the most successful in women's football. They are two-time <a href="/wiki/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="FIFA Women's World Cup">world champions</a>, having won the <a href="/wiki/2003_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2003 FIFA Women's World Cup">2003</a> and <a href="/wiki/2007_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2007 FIFA Women's World Cup">2007</a> tournaments. They are also the only nation to have won both the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">men's</a> and women's tournament. The team has won eight of the eleven <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Women%27s_Championship" title="UEFA Women's Championship">UEFA European Championships</a>, claiming the last six consecutive titles. Again, being the only nation to win both the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro" title="UEFA Euro">men's</a> and women's European tournament. Germany has won Olympic gold in <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2016_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_tournament" title="Football at the 2016 Summer Olympics – Women's tournament">2016</a>, after three consecutive bronze medals at the <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_Summer_Olympics" title="Football at the Summer Olympics">Women's Olympic Football Tournament</a>, finishing third in <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2000_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_tournament" title="Football at the 2000 Summer Olympics – Women's tournament">2000</a>, <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2004_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_tournament" title="Football at the 2004 Summer Olympics – Women's tournament">2004</a> and <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2008_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_tournament" title="Football at the 2008 Summer Olympics – Women's tournament">2008</a>. <a href="/wiki/Birgit_Prinz" title="Birgit Prinz">Birgit Prinz</a> holds the record for most appearances and is the team's all-time leading goalscorer. Prinz has also set international records; she has received the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Player_of_the_Year" title="FIFA World Player of the Year">FIFA World Player of the Year</a> award three times and is the joint second <a href="/wiki/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup#Overall_top_goalscorers" title="FIFA Women's World Cup">overall top goalscorer</a> at the Women's World Cup.</p>
<p>Women's football was long met with skepticism in Germany, and official matches were banned by the DFB until 1970. However, the women's national team has grown in popularity since winning the World Cup in 2003, as it was chosen as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Sportspersonality_of_the_year" title="German Sportspersonality of the year">Germany's Sports Team of the Year</a>. The current head coach is <a href="/wiki/Steffi_Jones" title="Steffi Jones">Steffi Jones</a>, replacing <a href="/wiki/Silvia_Neid" title="Silvia Neid">Silvia Neid</a> who was in charge from 2005 until 2016. As of December 2016, Germany is ranked No. 2 in the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Rankings" title="FIFA Women's World Rankings">FIFA Women's World Rankings</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germany_women%27s_national_football_team&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germany_women%27s_national_football_team&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1955, the <a href="/wiki/German_Football_Association" title="German Football Association">DFB</a> decided to forbid women's football in all its clubs in <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a>. In its explanation, the DFB cited that "this combative sport is fundamentally foreign to the nature of women" and that "body and soul would inevitably suffer damage". Further, the "display of the body violates etiquette and decency".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> In spite of this ban, more than 150 unofficial international matches were played in the 1950s and 1960s. On 30 October 1970, the ban on women's football was lifted at the DFB annual convention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geschichte_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Geschichte-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other football associations had already formed official women's national teams in the 1970s, the DFB long remained uninvolved in women's football. In 1981, DFB official <a href="/wiki/Horst_R._Schmidt" title="Horst R. Schmidt">Horst R. Schmidt</a> was invited to send a team to the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_World_Invitational_Tournament" title="Women's World Invitational Tournament">unofficial women's football world championship</a>. Schmidt accepted the invitation but hid the fact that West Germany had no women's national team at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geschichte_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Geschichte-3">[3]</a></sup> To avoid humiliation, the DFB sent the German club champions <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bergisch_Gladbach_09" title="Bergisch Gladbach 09">Bergisch Gladbach 09</a>, who went on to win the tournament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Seeing a need, the DFB established the women's national team in 1982. DFB president Hermann Neuberger appointed <a href="/wiki/Gero_Bisanz" title="Gero Bisanz">Gero Bisanz</a>, an instructor at the <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> Sports College, to set up the team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-welt_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-welt-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1982.E2.80.931994:_Difficult_beginnings_and_first_European_titles">1982–1994: Difficult beginnings and first European titles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germany_women%27s_national_football_team&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1982–1994: Difficult beginnings and first European titles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In September 1982, Bisanz organised two scouting training courses from which he selected a squad of 16 players.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sportschule_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-sportschule-6">[6]</a></sup> The team's first international match took place on 10 November 1982 in <a href="/wiki/Koblenz" title="Koblenz">Koblenz</a>. Following the tradition of the men’s team, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Switzerland women's national football team">Switzerland</a> was chosen as West Germany's first opponent. Doris Kresimon scored the first international goal in the 25th minute. In the second half, 18-year-old <a href="/wiki/Silvia_Neid" title="Silvia Neid">Silvia Neid</a> contributed two goals to the 5–1 victory; Neid later became the assistant coach in 1996 and the head coach in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-welt_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-welt-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>With five draws and one defeat, West Germany failed to qualify for the inaugural <a href="/wiki/1984_European_Competition_for_Women%27s_Football" title="1984 European Competition for Women's Football">1984 European Championship</a>, finishing third in the qualifying group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euro1984_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-euro1984-7">[7]</a></sup> In the beginning, Bisanz's primary objective was to close the gap to the <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian</a> countries and <a href="/wiki/Italy_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Italy women's national football team">Italy</a> – then the strongest teams in Europe. He emphasized training in basic skills and the need for an effective youth programme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teil1_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-teil1-8">[8]</a></sup> Starting in 1985, Bisanz increasingly called-up younger players, but at first had little success with this concept, as West Germany again failed to qualify for the <a href="/wiki/1987_European_Competition_for_Women%27s_Football" title="1987 European Competition for Women's Football">1987 European Championship</a> finals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euro1987_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-euro1987-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Undefeated and without conceding a goal, the German team qualified for the <a href="/wiki/1989_European_Competition_for_Women%27s_Football" title="1989 European Competition for Women's Football">European Championship</a> for the first time in 1989; the tournament was played on home soil in West Germany. The semi-final against Italy was the first international women's football match shown live on German television.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teil2_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-teil2-10">[10]</a></sup> The game was decided by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penalty_shootout_(association_football)" title="Penalty shootout (association football)">penalty shootout</a>, in which goalkeeper Marion Isbert saved three penalty kicks and scored the winning penalty herself. On 2 July 1989 in <a href="/wiki/Osnabr%C3%BCck" title="Osnabrück">Osnabrück</a>, West Germany played <a href="/wiki/Norway_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Norway women's national football team">Norway</a> in the final. Before a crowd of 22,000, they beat favourites Norway and won 4–1 with goals from Ursula Lohn, <a href="/wiki/Heidi_Mohr" title="Heidi Mohr">Heidi Mohr</a> and Angelika Fehrmann. This victory marked the team's first international title.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euro1989_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-euro1989-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/German_reunification" title="German reunification">German reunification</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_German" title="East German">East German</a> football association joined the DFB. The East German women's national football team had played only one official international match, losing 0–3 to Czechoslovakia in a friendly match on 9 May 1990. The unified German team defended their title successfully at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1991_UEFA_Women%27s_Championship" title="1991 UEFA Women's Championship">1991 European Championship</a>. After winning all games in the qualifying group, Germany again met Italy in the semi-final, this time winning 3–0. On 14 July 1991, the German team once more faced Norway in the final. The game went to extra time, during which Heidi Mohr and Silvia Neid scored for Germany and secured the 3–1 victory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euro1991_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-euro1991-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 1991, Germany participated in the first <a href="/wiki/1991_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="1991 FIFA Women's World Cup">Women's World Cup</a> in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. Following victories over <a href="/wiki/Nigeria_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Nigeria women's national football team">Nigeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Taipei_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Chinese Taipei women's national football team">Taiwan</a> and Italy, the German team reached the quarter-final without conceding a single goal. Silvia Neid scored the first German World Cup goal on 17 November 1991 against Nigeria. Germany won the quarter-final against Denmark 2–1 after extra time, but lost 2–5 in the semi-final to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_women%27s_national_soccer_team" title="United States women's national soccer team">United States</a>, who went on to win the tournament. Following a 0–4 defeat in the third-place match against <a href="/wiki/Sweden_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Sweden women's national football team">Sweden</a>, Germany finished fourth in the tournament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc1991_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-wc1991-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The German team failed to defend their title at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1993_UEFA_Women%27s_Championship" title="1993 UEFA Women's Championship">1993 European Championship</a>, suffering a semi-final defeat to Italy in a penalty shootout, and later losing 1–3 against Denmark in the third-place playoff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euro1993_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-euro1993-14">[14]</a></sup> Despite the disappointing result, new talents such as <a href="/wiki/Steffi_Jones" title="Steffi Jones">Steffi Jones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maren_Meinert" title="Maren Meinert">Maren Meinert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silke_Rottenberg" title="Silke Rottenberg">Silke Rottenberg</a> made their tournament debut and later became key players for the German team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teil2_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-teil2-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1995.E2.80.932002:_Olympic_and_World_Cup_disappointments">1995–2002: Olympic and World Cup disappointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germany_women%27s_national_football_team&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1995–2002: Olympic and World Cup disappointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Birgit_Prinz" title="Birgit Prinz">Birgit Prinz</a> scored in a major tournament for the first time in 1995. In 1995, Germany won its third <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1995_UEFA_Women%27s_Championship" title="1995 UEFA Women's Championship">European Championship</a>. After winning all qualification matches, scoring 55 goals, the German team defeated <a href="/wiki/England_women%27s_national_football_team" title="England women's national football team">England</a> 6–2 over two legs in the semi-final. Germany met Sweden in the final, which was played at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fritz_Walter_Stadion" title="Fritz Walter Stadion">Fritz Walter Stadion</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kaiserslautern" title="Kaiserslautern">Kaiserslautern</a>, Germany, on 26 March 1995. The Swedish team managed to score early, but Germany came back to win 3–2 with goals from Maren Meinert, <a href="/wiki/Birgit_Prinz" title="Birgit Prinz">Birgit Prinz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bettina_Wiegmann" title="Bettina Wiegmann">Bettina Wiegmann</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euro1995_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-euro1995-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/1995_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="1995 FIFA Women's World Cup">1995 Women's World Cup</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, the German team lost against the Scandinavian hosts, but still succeeded in winning their group by beating <a href="/wiki/Japan_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Japan women's national football team">Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brazil_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Brazil women's national football team">Brazil</a>. Germany won the quarter-final against England 3–0, and defeated <a href="/wiki/China_women%27s_national_football_team" title="China women's national football team">China</a> 1–0 with a late goal by Bettina Wiegmann in the semi-final. On 18 June 1995 in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>, the German team appeared in their first Women's World Cup final. Facing Norway, they lost the match 0–2, but as runners-up achieved their best World Cup result until then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc1995_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-wc1995-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Women’s football was first played as an Olympic sport at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Football_at_the_1996_Summer_Olympics_-_Women%27s_tournament_matches" title="Football at the 1996 Summer Olympics - Women's tournament matches">1996 Summer Olympics</a>. Bettina Wiegmann scored the first Olympic goal in the opening match against Japan, which Germany won 3–2. After losing their second group game against Norway 2–3, and drawing with Brazil 1–1, Germany was eliminated, finishing third in the group with four points from three matches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olymp1996_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-olymp1996-17">[17]</a></sup> Head coach Gero Bisanz resigned after the tournament and his assistant since 1983, <a href="/wiki/Tina_Theune" title="Tina Theune">Tina Theune</a>, took over as the new national coach. Silvia Neid ended her playing career and was appointed the new assistant coach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meyer_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-meyer-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1997_UEFA_Women%27s_Championship" title="1997 UEFA Women's Championship">1997 European Championship</a> was the first test for new coach Theune. Following a defeat against Norway, Germany finished second in the qualifying group and only secured qualification by beating <a href="/wiki/Iceland_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Iceland women's national football team">Iceland</a> in a relegation play-off. After drawing with Italy and Norway, a victory over Denmark in the last group game saw the German team go through to the knockout stage. They beat Sweden 1–0 in the semi-final, and on 12 July 1997, claimed their fourth European championship with a 2–0 win over Italy, with goals from <a href="/wiki/Sandra_Minnert" title="Sandra Minnert">Sandra Minnert</a> and Birgit Prinz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euro1997_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-euro1997-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/1999_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="1999 FIFA Women's World Cup">1999 Women's World Cup</a> in the United States, the German team also failed to qualify directly, but managed to beat the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Ukraine women's national football team">Ukraine</a> in a qualifying play-off. Germany started their World Cup campaign by drawing with Italy and winning 6–0 over <a href="/wiki/Mexico_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Mexico women's national football team">Mexico</a>. In the last group game, Germany drew 3–3 against Brazil; by conceding a last minute equalizer, Germany failed to win the group and subsequently had to face the hosts in the quarter-final. With 54,642 people in attendance, among them U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>, the crowd at the <a href="/wiki/FedExField" title="FedExField">Jack Kent Cooke Stadium</a> was the biggest the German team had ever played in front of. Despite leading twice, they lost 2–3 to the eventual World Cup winners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc1999_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-wc1999-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Germany competed at the <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2000_Summer_Olympics" title="Football at the 2000 Summer Olympics">2000 Summer Olympics</a>, winning all three group games against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australia_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Australia women's national football team">Australia</a>, Brazil and Sweden. The German team dominated the semi-final against Norway, but lost the game 0–1 after an <a href="/wiki/Own_goal" title="Own goal">own goal</a> by <a href="/wiki/Tina_Wunderlich" title="Tina Wunderlich">Tina Wunderlich</a> in the 80th minute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> They beat Brazil 2–0 in the third place match with goals from Birgit Prinz and <a href="/wiki/Renate_Lingor" title="Renate Lingor">Renate Lingor</a>, and won the bronze medal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olymp2000_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-olymp2000-22">[22]</a></sup> It was the first Olympic medal for the German Football Associations since 1988 when the men's team also won bronze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2001, Germany hosted the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2001_UEFA_Women%27s_Championship" title="2001 UEFA Women's Championship">European Championship</a>. Following victories over Sweden, <a href="/wiki/Russia_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Russia women's national football team">Russia</a> and England in the group stage, the German team beat Norway 1–0 in the semi-final courtesy of a diving header by <a href="/wiki/Sandra_Smisek" title="Sandra Smisek">Sandra Smisek</a>. On 7 July 2001 in <a href="/wiki/Ulm" title="Ulm">Ulm</a>, they met Sweden in the final, which was played in heavy rain. The game was scoreless after 90 minutes and went to extra time, where <a href="/wiki/Claudia_M%C3%BCller" title="Claudia Müller">Claudia Müller</a> scored a <a href="/wiki/Golden_goal" title="Golden goal">golden goal</a> and secured the fifth European title for Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euro2001_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-euro2001-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2003.E2.80.93present:_Two_consecutive_World_Cup_titles">2003–present: Two consecutive World Cup titles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germany_women%27s_national_football_team&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 2003–present: Two consecutive World Cup titles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup_2003_-_Germany_vs_Sweden.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup_2003_-_Germany_vs_Sweden.jpg/220px-FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup_2003_-_Germany_vs_Sweden.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup_2003_-_Germany_vs_Sweden.jpg/330px-FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup_2003_-_Germany_vs_Sweden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup_2003_-_Germany_vs_Sweden.jpg/440px-FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup_2003_-_Germany_vs_Sweden.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup_2003_-_Germany_vs_Sweden.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Germany playing <a href="/wiki/Sweden_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Sweden women's national football team">Sweden</a> in the <a href="/wiki/2003_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2003 FIFA Women's World Cup">2003 Women's World Cup</a> final.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/2003_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2003 FIFA Women's World Cup">2003 Women's World Cup</a> in the United States, Germany was drawn in a group with <a href="/wiki/Canada_women%27s_national_soccer_team" title="Canada women's national soccer team">Canada</a>, Japan and <a href="/wiki/Argentina_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Argentina women's national football team">Argentina</a>. After winning all three group games, the German team defeated Russia 7–1 in the quarter-final, which set up another clash with the United States. Germany's <a href="/wiki/Kerstin_Garefrekes" title="Kerstin Garefrekes">Kerstin Garefrekes</a> scored after 15 minutes and goalkeeper Silke Rottenberg made several key saves. In the dying minutes of the semi-final, Maren Meinert and Birgit Prinz sealed the 3–0 win. On 12 October 2003, Germany met Sweden in the World Cup final in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>. The Scandinavians went ahead before half time, but Maren Meinert equalized shortly after the break. The game went to extra time, where <a href="/wiki/Nia_K%C3%BCnzer" title="Nia Künzer">Nia Künzer</a> headed the winning golden goal in the 98th minute to claim Germany's first Women's World Cup title.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc2003_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-wc2003-25">[25]</a></sup> Birgit Prinz was honoured as the tournament's best player and top goalscorer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>With wins over China and Mexico, the German team finished first in their group at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2004_Summer_Olympics_-_Women%27s_tournament" title="Football at the 2004 Summer Olympics - Women's tournament">2004 Summer Olympics</a>. They beat Nigeria 2–1 in the quarter-final, but suffered a 1–2 semi-final loss to the United States after extra time. In the third place match, Germany defeated Sweden 1–0 with a goal by Renate Lingor, winning the teams's second Olympic bronze medal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olymp2004_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-olymp2004-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2005_UEFA_Women%27s_Championship" title="2005 UEFA Women's Championship">2005 European Championship</a> was held in England. With wins over Norway, Italy and <a href="/wiki/France_women%27s_national_football_team" title="France women's national football team">France</a> in Round 1, the German team advanced to the semi-final, where they defeated <a href="/wiki/Finland_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Finland women's national football team">Finland</a> 4–1. On 19 June 2005, they met Norway for the third time in the European championship final. Germany won 3–1 with goals from <a href="/wiki/Inka_Grings" title="Inka Grings">Inka Grings</a>, Renate Lingor and Birgit Prinz and added a sixth European title.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euro2005_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-euro2005-28">[28]</a></sup> Head coach Tina Theune stepped down after the tournament and her assistant Silvia Neid took over as national coach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meyer_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-meyer-18">[18]</a></sup> In 2006, Germany won the annual <a href="/wiki/Algarve_Cup" title="Algarve Cup">Algarve Cup</a> for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-algarve_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-algarve-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nadine_Angerer_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="185" data-file-width="300" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Nadine_Angerer_01.jpg/220px-Nadine_Angerer_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ad/Nadine_Angerer_01.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nadine_Angerer_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Nadine_Angerer" title="Nadine Angerer">Nadine Angerer</a> saved a penalty in the <a href="/wiki/2007_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2007 FIFA Women's World Cup">2007 Women's World Cup</a> final.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As reigning world champion, Germany played the opening game at the <a href="/wiki/2007_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2007 FIFA Women's World Cup">2007 Women's World Cup</a> in China, outclassing Argentina 11–0. After a goalless draw against England and a 2–0 win over Japan, the German team defeated <a href="/wiki/North_Korea_women%27s_national_football_team" title="North Korea women's national football team">North Korea</a> 3–0 in the quarter-final. They beat Norway by the same result in the semi-final, with goals from <a href="/wiki/Kerstin_Stegemann" title="Kerstin Stegemann">Kerstin Stegemann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martina_M%C3%BCller_(footballer)" title="Martina Müller (footballer)">Martina Müller</a> and a Norwegian own goal. On 30 September 2007, Germany faced Brazil in the World Cup final in <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>. Birgit Prinz put Germany in front after half time and goalkeeper <a href="/wiki/Nadine_Angerer" title="Nadine Angerer">Nadine Angerer</a> saved a penalty by Brazilian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marta_Vieira_da_Silva" title="Marta Vieira da Silva">Marta</a>. <a href="/wiki/Simone_Laudehr" title="Simone Laudehr">Simone Laudehr</a> scored a second goal after 86 minutes, which sealed the German 2–0 victory. Germany was the first team (men's and women's game) to win the World Cup without conceding a goal and the first to successfully defend the Women's World Cup title.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc2007_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-wc2007-30">[30]</a></sup> With 14 goals, Prinz became the tournament's <a href="/wiki/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup#Overall_top_goalscorers" title="FIFA Women's World Cup">overall top goalscorer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc-records_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-wc-records-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a replay of the 2007 World Cup final, the German team drew 0–0 with Brazil in the opening game at the <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2008_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_tournament" title="Football at the 2008 Summer Olympics – Women's tournament">2008 Summer Olympics</a>. They then beat both Nigeria and North Korea to advance to the quarter-final, where they defeated Sweden 2–0 after extra time. In the semi-final, Germany again met Brazil. Birgit Prinz scored in the 10th minute, but the German team lost 1–4 after conceding three goals to Brazilian counter-attacks in the second half. They beat Japan 2–0 for the bronze medal, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatmire_Bajramaj" title="Fatmire Bajramaj">Fatmire Bajramaj</a> scoring both goals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olymp2008_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-olymp2008-32">[32]</a></sup> The third consecutive semi-final loss at the Olympics was seen as a disappointment by both the players and the German press.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The team's overall performance and head coach Silvia Neid were harshly criticised in the media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Germany qualified for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2009_UEFA_Women%27s_Championship" title="2009 UEFA Women's Championship">2009 European Championship</a> in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> winning all eight games and scoring 34 goals. They beat Norway, France and Iceland in the group stage to advance to the quarter-final, where they won 2–1 against Italy. After trailing Norway at half-time in the semi-final, the German team fought back to a 3–1 victory. On 10 September 2009, they defeated England 6–2 for their seventh European trophy. Birgit Prinz and Inka Grings scored twice, with <a href="/wiki/Melanie_Behringer" title="Melanie Behringer">Melanie Behringer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kim_Kulig" title="Kim Kulig">Kim Kulig</a> also scoring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euro2009_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-euro2009-35">[35]</a></sup> Grings retained her award as the tournament's top scorer from 2005, while Germany extended their winning streak at the European Championship finals to a 19-match run dating back to 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup_Canada_2015_-_Edmonton_(18821505113).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2266" data-file-width="4413" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup_Canada_2015_-_Edmonton_%2818821505113%29.jpg/220px-FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup_Canada_2015_-_Edmonton_%2818821505113%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup_Canada_2015_-_Edmonton_%2818821505113%29.jpg/330px-FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup_Canada_2015_-_Edmonton_%2818821505113%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup_Canada_2015_-_Edmonton_%2818821505113%29.jpg/440px-FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup_Canada_2015_-_Edmonton_%2818821505113%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup_Canada_2015_-_Edmonton_(18821505113).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Fara_Williams" title="Fara Williams">Fara Williams</a> calmly slots a <a href="/wiki/Penalty_kick_(association_football)" title="Penalty kick (association football)">penalty</a> beyond <a href="/wiki/Nadine_Angerer" title="Nadine Angerer">Nadine Angerer</a> at the <a href="/wiki/2015_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2015 FIFA Women's World Cup">2015 FIFA Women's World Cup</a>, to inflict Germany's first ever defeat by England</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2011, Germany was host of the <a href="/wiki/2011_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2011 FIFA Women's World Cup">2011 FIFA Women's World Cup</a>. The German team won the three games on the group stage, over Canada, France and Nigeria. On the quarterfinals, the team suffered an upset by Japan, who won on overtime with a goal by <a href="/wiki/Karina_Maruyama" title="Karina Maruyama">Karina Maruyama</a>. The defeat broke the Germans' streak of sixteen undefeated games at the World Cup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc2011_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-wc2011-37">[37]</a></sup> By failing to finish among the top two UEFA teams, Germany was unable to qualify for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2012_Summer_Olympics_-_Women%27s_tournament" title="Football at the 2012 Summer Olympics - Women's tournament">2012 Summer Olympics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2013_UEFA_Women%27s_Championship" title="2013 UEFA Women's Championship">2013 European Championship</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, the Germans won their sixth straight continental title, with the decisive game being a 1-0 victory over Norway. Goalkeeper Nadine Angerer, who stopped two penalties during the final, was chosen as the tournament's best player.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/2015_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2015 FIFA Women's World Cup">2015 FIFA Women's World Cup</a> had Germany again reaching the top four. In the semifinal against the United States, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celia_Sasic" title="Celia Sasic">Celia Sasic</a>, who wound up as the tournament's top scorer, missed a penalty, and afterwards goals by <a href="/wiki/Carli_Lloyd" title="Carli Lloyd">Carli Lloyd</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kelley_O%27Hara" title="Kelley O'Hara">Kelley O'Hara</a> lead to an American victory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The third place match saw the Germans lose their first ever match to England after 21 contests, due to a penalty kick by <a href="/wiki/Fara_Williams" title="Fara Williams">Fara Williams</a> during extra time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tournament_record">Tournament record</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germany_women%27s_national_football_team&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Tournament record">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_Cup">World Cup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germany_women%27s_national_football_team&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: World Cup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Germany is one of the most successful nations at the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="FIFA Women's World Cup">FIFA Women's World Cup</a>, having won the tournament twice and finishing runner-up once.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The German team won the World Cup in 2003 and 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc2003_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-wc2003-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc2007_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-wc2007-30">[30]</a></sup> At the first World Cup in 1991, they finished in fourth place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc1991_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-wc1991-13">[13]</a></sup> In 1995, Germany reached the World Cup final, but were defeated by <a href="/wiki/Norway_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Norway women's national football team">Norway</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc1995_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-wc1995-16">[16]</a></sup> The team's worst results were quarter-final losses to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_women%27s_national_soccer_team" title="United States women's national soccer team">United States</a> in 1999,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc1999_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-wc1999-20">[20]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Japan_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Japan women's national football team">Japan</a> in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc2011_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-wc2011-37">[37]</a></sup> Overall, the German team has appeared in three Women's World Cup finals, and is a five-time semi-finalist. They have participated in every Women's World Cup and have a 26–5–8 win–draw–loss record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc-records_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-wc-records-31">[31]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Olympic_Games">Olympic Games</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germany_women%27s_national_football_team&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Olympic Games">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Women's football debuted at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Football_at_the_1996_Summer_Olympics_-_Women%27s_tournament_matches" title="Football at the 1996 Summer Olympics - Women's tournament matches">1996 Summer Olympics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bettina_Wiegmann" title="Bettina Wiegmann">Bettina Wiegmann</a> scored the first Olympic goal in the opening game of the tournament. However, Germany failed to progress to the knockout stage and was eliminated after Round 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olymp1996_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-olymp1996-17">[17]</a></sup> Four years later the German team won the bronze medal at the <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2000_Summer_Olympics" title="Football at the 2000 Summer Olympics">2000 Summer Olympics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olymp2000_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-olymp2000-22">[22]</a></sup> They again finished third at both the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2004_Summer_Olympics_-_Women%27s_tournament" title="Football at the 2004 Summer Olympics - Women's tournament">2004</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2008_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_tournament" title="Football at the 2008 Summer Olympics – Women's tournament">2008 Summer Olympics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olymp2004_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-olymp2004-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olymp2008_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-olymp2008-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>The German team has qualified for all <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_Summer_Olympics" title="Football at the Summer Olympics">Women's Olympic Football Tournaments</a> until 2008. However, they failed to qualify for the <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2012_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_tournament" title="Football at the 2012 Summer Olympics – Women's tournament">2012 tournament</a> as UEFA used the 2011 World Cup for qualification, and Germany ended below <a href="/wiki/France_women%27s_national_football_team" title="France women's national football team">France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sweden_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Sweden women's national football team">Sweden</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The German team beat Sweden in the Olympics final in Rio in 2016 to obtain their first Olympic gold medal. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Championship">European Championship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germany_women%27s_national_football_team&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: European Championship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Germany failed to qualify for the first two <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Women%27s_Championship" title="UEFA Women's Championship">UEFA European Championships</a> in 1984 and 1987.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euro1984_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-euro1984-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euro1987_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-euro1987-9">[9]</a></sup> Since 1989, the German team has participated in every tournament and is the record European champion with eight titles. Germany has won the last six consecutive championships and has an overall 32–5–2 win–draw–loss record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euro2005_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-euro2005-28">[28]</a></sup> The worst German result at the European championship finals was finishing fourth in 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euro1993_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-euro1993-14">[14]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coaches">Coaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germany_women%27s_national_football_team&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Coaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Former German international Silvia Neid is the current head coach of the German women's national football team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coaches_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-coaches-45">[45]</a></sup> As a player, she won 111 <a href="/wiki/Cap_(sport)" title="Cap (sport)">caps</a> and scored 48 goals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goals_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-goals-46">[46]</a></sup> The coach's official title is <i>DFB-Trainer</i> and he or she is employed by the German Football Association.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coaches_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-coaches-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silvia_Neid_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="282" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Silvia_Neid_02.jpg/220px-Silvia_Neid_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e3/Silvia_Neid_02.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silvia_Neid_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Former head coach <a href="/wiki/Silvia_Neid" title="Silvia Neid">Silvia Neid</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><b>Gero Bisanz</b> was the first coach of the women's national team. He selected his first squad in September 1982.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teil1_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-teil1-8">[8]</a></sup> At the same time, he also worked as the chief instructor for DFB coaching training from 1971 to 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sportschule_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-sportschule-6">[6]</a></sup> Bisanz led the German team to three <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Women%27s_Championship" title="UEFA Women's Championship">European Championships</a> in 1989, 1991 and 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uefa_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-uefa-47">[47]</a></sup> Under Bisanz, Germany also was runner-up at the <a href="/wiki/1995_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="1995 FIFA Women's World Cup">1995 Women's World Cup</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc1995_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-wc1995-16">[16]</a></sup> He resigned after the German team was eliminated in Round 1 at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Football_at_the_1996_Summer_Olympics_-_Women%27s_tournament_matches" title="Football at the 1996 Summer Olympics - Women's tournament matches">1996 Summer Olympics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> With his assistant since 1983, Tina Theune, he built a scouting system and was responsible for a new DFB youth programme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teil1_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-teil1-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Tina Theune</b> took over as head coach after the 1996 Summer Olympics. She was the first woman to acquire the highest German football coaching license.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meyer_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-meyer-18">[18]</a></sup> Theune was responsible for three European Championship titles in 1997, 2001 and 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uefa_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-uefa-47">[47]</a></sup> During her time as head coach, Germany won the bronze medal at the <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2000_Summer_Olympics" title="Football at the 2000 Summer Olympics">2000</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2004_Summer_Olympics_-_Women%27s_tournament" title="Football at the 2004 Summer Olympics - Women's tournament">2004 Summer Olympics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Her biggest success was the <a href="/wiki/2003_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2003 FIFA Women's World Cup">2003 Women's World Cup</a> title.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc2003_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-wc2003-25">[25]</a></sup> Theune is the most successful national coach to date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meyer_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-meyer-18">[18]</a></sup> She benefited from an effective youth programme and integrated several Under-19 players into the nation team. Theune stepped down after winning the European Championship in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meyer_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-meyer-18">[18]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Silvia Neid</b> was the team's assistant coach from 1996 to 2005 and the head coach of the German Under-19 team, who won the <a href="/wiki/2004_FIFA_U-19_Women%27s_World_Championship" title="2004 FIFA U-19 Women's World Championship">2004 U-19 Women's World Championship</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In July 2005, she became the team's head coach and the 2006 <a href="/wiki/Algarve_Cup" title="Algarve Cup">Algarve Cup</a> marked her first tournament win.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-algarve_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-algarve-29">[29]</a></sup> By winning the <a href="/wiki/2007_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2007 FIFA Women's World Cup">2007 Women’s World Cup</a>, Neid became the first German national coach (men's and women’s team) to win the World Cup at the first attempt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc2007_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-wc2007-30">[30]</a></sup> At her first Summer Olympics as a coach in 2008, Germany won the bronze medal for a third time. Neid was also responsible for Germany's seventh European Championship in 2009. She is signed until 2016 and her assistant is Ulrike Ballweg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coaches_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-coaches-45">[45]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>On 30 March 2015 it was announced that <a href="/wiki/Steffi_Jones" title="Steffi Jones">Steffi Jones</a> would become the new German head coach in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Venues">Venues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germany_women%27s_national_football_team&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Venues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" style="font-size: 92%; float:right; margin:0 0 0.5em 1em;">

<p>The German national football team has no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_stadium" title="National stadium">national stadium</a>. Like the men, the women's team play their home matches in different stadiums throughout the country. As of June 2011, they have played in 87 different German cities. Most home games have been held in <a href="/wiki/Osnabr%C3%BCck" title="Osnabrück">Osnabrück</a> with six matches, followed by <a href="/wiki/Ulm" title="Ulm">Ulm</a> (five games), and <a href="/wiki/Bochum" title="Bochum">Bochum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kaiserslautern" title="Kaiserslautern">Kaiserslautern</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koblenz" title="Koblenz">Koblenz</a>, <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCdenscheid" title="Lüdenscheid">Lüdenscheid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rheine" title="Rheine">Rheine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siegen" title="Siegen">Siegen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weil_am_Rhein" title="Weil am Rhein">Weil am Rhein</a> (three games each).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-games_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-games-52">[52]</a></sup> The first home match in former <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a> was played in <a href="/wiki/Aue" title="Aue">Aue</a> in May 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Germany-Brazil.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Germany-Brazil.jpg/220px-Germany-Brazil.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Germany-Brazil.jpg/330px-Germany-Brazil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Germany-Brazil.jpg/440px-Germany-Brazil.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Germany-Brazil.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Germany playing <a href="/wiki/Brazil_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Brazil women's national football team">Brazil</a> before a crowd of 44,825 in <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 1980s and 1990s, home matches were mostly played in smaller towns with no professional football clubs. As the team became more successful, especially after the World Cup win in 2003, the number of spectators rose accordingly. Today, the team usually plays in stadiums with 10,000 to 25,000 seats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-portrait_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-portrait-55">[55]</a></sup> The ten largest German cities have only hosted five international matches. The team have played twice in <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>, and once <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bremen" title="Bremen">Bremen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dortmund" title="Dortmund">Dortmund</a>, <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="Düsseldorf">Düsseldorf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Essen" title="Essen">Essen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart" title="Stuttgart">Stuttgart</a> have never hosted an international match of the women's team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-games_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-games-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Outside Germany, they have played the most games in <a href="/wiki/Faro,_Portugal" title="Faro, Portugal">Faro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> (10 matches), and <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">China</a> (six matches), the host cities of the annual <a href="/wiki/Algarve_Cup" title="Algarve Cup">Algarve Cup</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Four_Nations_Tournament_(women%27s_football)" title="Four Nations Tournament (women's football)">Four Nations Tournament</a> respectively. They have also played five games in <a href="/wiki/Albufeira" title="Albufeira">Albufeira</a>, Portugal (also an Algarve Cup venue), and four times in <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis" title="Minneapolis">Minneapolis</a> in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-games_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-games-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>The record attendance for Germany was 73,680 in the <a href="/wiki/2011_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2011 FIFA Women's World Cup">2011 Women's World Cup</a> opening game against <a href="/wiki/Canada_women%27s_national_soccer_team" title="Canada women's national soccer team">Canada</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Stadium" title="Olympic Stadium">Olympic Stadium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> That game also set a new European record in women's football. Away from home, the team's crowd record was 54,642 in the <a href="/wiki/1999_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="1999 FIFA Women's World Cup">1999 Women's World Cup</a> quarter-final against the <a href="/wiki/United_States_women%27s_national_soccer_team" title="United States women's national soccer team">United States</a> at the <a href="/wiki/FedExField" title="FedExField">Jack Kent Cooke Stadium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Landover,_Maryland" title="Landover, Maryland">Landover</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Colours">Colours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germany_women%27s_national_football_team&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Colours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:67px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_arms_of_Germany.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="500" height="85" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Coat_of_arms_of_Germany.svg/65px-Coat_of_arms_of_Germany.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Coat_of_arms_of_Germany.svg/98px-Coat_of_arms_of_Germany.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Coat_of_arms_of_Germany.svg/130px-Coat_of_arms_of_Germany.svg.png 2x" width="65"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_arms_of_Germany.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Emblem for the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The German women's national football team wears white shirts with black shorts and white socks, following the tradition of the <a href="/wiki/Germany_national_football_team" title="Germany national football team">German men's team</a> – black and white are the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Prussia" title="Flag of Prussia">colours of Prussia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The current change kit is red and black, with black shorts and red socks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trikot_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-trikot-59">[59]</a></sup> In the past, Germany also used green shirts with white shorts and green socks as the away kit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>The women's national team originally played with the emblem of the German men's team, a variation of the DFB logo with the Federal Eagle of Germany (<i>Bundesadler</i>) and three <a href="/wiki/Star_(football_badge)" title="Star (football badge)">stars at the top</a> for the men's <a href="/wiki/1954_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1954 FIFA World Cup">1954</a>, <a href="/wiki/1974_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1974 FIFA World Cup">1974</a> and <a href="/wiki/1990_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1990 FIFA World Cup">1990</a> <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">World Cup</a> titles. Since their first <a href="/wiki/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="FIFA Women's World Cup">Women's World Cup</a> win in 2003, the team displays its own World Cup titles; initially with one star,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> and since 2007, with two stars at the top of the emblem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-broschure_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-broschure-62">[62]</a></sup> While being reigning world champions, Germany also displayed the newly created "FIFA Women's World Champions Badge" on their shirts from 2009 until 2011 when they were succeeded by Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BluehImGlanzeDiesesGlueckes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="148" data-file-width="800" height="41" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/BluehImGlanzeDiesesGlueckes.jpg/220px-BluehImGlanzeDiesesGlueckes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/BluehImGlanzeDiesesGlueckes.jpg/330px-BluehImGlanzeDiesesGlueckes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/BluehImGlanzeDiesesGlueckes.jpg/440px-BluehImGlanzeDiesesGlueckes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BluehImGlanzeDiesesGlueckes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Verse of the <a href="/wiki/Deutschlandlied" title="Deutschlandlied">national anthem</a> on the collar.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In accordance with the rules of the <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">International Olympic Committee</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Germany does not wear its official uniform with the logo of the German Football Association while competing at the Summer Olympics. Instead, the DFB badge is replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Germany" title="Coat of arms of Germany">coat of arms of Germany</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-broschure_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-broschure-62">[62]</a></sup> Like all DFB squads, the women's national team is supplied by <a href="/wiki/Adidas" title="Adidas">Adidas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trikot_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-trikot-59">[59]</a></sup> which had provided a specifically designed female football jersey since 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kleiders.C3.A4cke_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-kleiders.C3.A4cke-65">[65]</a></sup> The team's main sponsor is the German insurance company <a href="/wiki/Allianz" title="Allianz">Allianz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Acceptance_and_popularity">Acceptance and popularity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germany_women%27s_national_football_team&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Acceptance and popularity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For most of the 20th century, women's football was a niche sport in Germany and was frowned upon. When the DFB appointed <a href="/wiki/Gero_Bisanz" title="Gero Bisanz">Gero Bisanz</a> to coach the newly founded women's national team, he was initially very reluctant about his assignment and feared it would harm his reputation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-portrait_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-portrait-55">[55]</a></sup> Winning the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1989_UEFA_Women%27s_Championship" title="1989 UEFA Women's Championship">1989 European Championship</a> was the team's first international success, but it had little lasting effect on their popularity. As a gift for the first European trophy, every player received a <a href="/wiki/Tea_set" title="Tea set">tea set</a>, which is often cited as an example of male chauvinism and general lack of interest in the women's national team at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-portrait_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-portrait-55">[55]</a></sup> This attitude within the German Football Association has changed considerably in the last two decades, in particular during the term of <a href="/wiki/Theo_Zwanziger" title="Theo Zwanziger">Theo Zwanziger</a> as DFB president, an outspoken supporter of women's football.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taz_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-taz-67">[67]</a></sup> Each member of the <a href="/wiki/2003_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2003 FIFA Women's World Cup">2003 Women's World Cup</a> squad received a prearranged bonus of 15,000 <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">euros</a> for winning the tournament; four years later the players received 50,000 euros for their successful title defense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In 2009, one million of the 6.7 million DFB members were female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 2003 World Cup title marked the breakthrough for the women’s national football team in Germany. The final was watched by 10.48 million viewers on German television (a 33.2 percent market share)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quoten_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-quoten-70">[70]</a></sup> and the German team was welcomed home by almost 10,000 fans at <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B6mer" title="Römer">Frankfurt's city hall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fifa_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-fifa-71">[71]</a></sup> Later that year, they were honoured as the 2003 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Sportspersonality_of_the_year" title="German Sportspersonality of the year">German Sports Team of the Year</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nia_K%C3%BCnzer" title="Nia Künzer">Nia Künzer</a>'s World Cup winning <a href="/wiki/Golden_goal" title="Golden goal">golden goal</a> was voted Germany's 2003 <a href="/wiki/Goal_of_the_Year_(Germany)" title="Goal of the Year (Germany)">Goal of the Year</a>, the first time the award was won by a female player.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Since 2005, almost all of the women’s national football team's matches have been shown live on German television.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roemerbalkon-weltmeisterinnen-empfang2007-001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2136" data-file-width="2848" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Roemerbalkon-weltmeisterinnen-empfang2007-001.jpg/220px-Roemerbalkon-weltmeisterinnen-empfang2007-001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Roemerbalkon-weltmeisterinnen-empfang2007-001.jpg/330px-Roemerbalkon-weltmeisterinnen-empfang2007-001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Roemerbalkon-weltmeisterinnen-empfang2007-001.jpg/440px-Roemerbalkon-weltmeisterinnen-empfang2007-001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roemerbalkon-weltmeisterinnen-empfang2007-001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Arrival in <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a> after winning the <a href="/wiki/2007_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2007 FIFA Women's World Cup">2007 Women's World Cup</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The final of the <a href="/wiki/2007_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2007 FIFA Women's World Cup">2007 Women's World Cup</a> was seen by 9.05 million television viewers (a 50.5 percent market share).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quoten_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-quoten-70">[70]</a></sup> After the team returned to Germany, they were celebrated by a crowd of 20,000 in Frankfurt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fifa_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-fifa-71">[71]</a></sup> In December 2007, all players of the World Cup squad received the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silberne_Lorbeerblatt" title="Silberne Lorbeerblatt">Silberne Lorbeerblatt</a> (Silver Laurel Leaf), the highest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_decoration" title="State decoration">state decoration</a> for athletes in Germany. National coach <a href="/wiki/Silvia_Neid" title="Silvia Neid">Silvia Neid</a> was awarded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bundesverdienstkreuz" title="Bundesverdienstkreuz">Federal Cross of Merit on ribbon</a> by German president <a href="/wiki/Horst_K%C3%B6hler" title="Horst Köhler">Horst Köhler</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009, the team's six home matches had an average attendance of 22,753.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In a survey of German football fans, 65 percent of the male and 62 percent of the female respondents said they were interested in women's football.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> However, this popularity is mostly limited to international matches. Although the number of spectators in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fu%C3%9Fball-Bundesliga_(women)" title="Fußball-Bundesliga (women)">women's Bundesliga</a> has more than doubled since 2003,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> the average attendance in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fu%C3%9Fball-Bundesliga_(women)_2007-08" title="Fußball-Bundesliga (women) 2007-08">2007–08 season</a> (887)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> was still less than three percent of that of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fu%C3%9Fball-Bundesliga" title="Fußball-Bundesliga">men's Bundesliga</a> (38,612).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Today, women's football is socially accepted in Germany, although one of the main points of criticism remains the alleged lack of quality compared to the men’s game. The German women’s national team has played several exhibition matches against male teams, most notably losing 0–3 to the <a href="/wiki/VfB_Stuttgart" title="VfB Stuttgart">VfB Stuttgart</a> Under-17 squad in preparation for the 2003 World Cup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taz_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-taz-67">[67]</a></sup> Most German players dismiss comparisons between the quality of men's and women's football; <a href="/wiki/Renate_Lingor" title="Renate Lingor">Renate Lingor</a> has said they are "two entirely different sports".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Players such as <a href="/wiki/Simone_Laudehr" title="Simone Laudehr">Simone Laudehr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ariane_Hingst" title="Ariane Hingst">Ariane Hingst</a> and <a href="/wiki/Melanie_Behringer" title="Melanie Behringer">Melanie Behringer</a> have stated that men’s football is played at a much faster pace, but also has more interruptions and brutal tackling than the women's game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kleiders.C3.A4cke_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-kleiders.C3.A4cke-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wdr_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-wdr-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Linda_Bresonik" title="Linda Bresonik">Linda Bresonik</a> has said she generally prefers to watch men's football.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wdr_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-wdr-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Results_and_fixtures">Results and fixtures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germany_women%27s_national_football_team&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Results and fixtures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Records">Records</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germany_women%27s_national_football_team&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Records">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kerstin_Stegemann_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="299" data-file-width="295" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Kerstin_Stegemann_3.jpg/220px-Kerstin_Stegemann_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a1/Kerstin_Stegemann_3.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kerstin_Stegemann_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Kerstin_Stegemann" title="Kerstin Stegemann">Kerstin Stegemann</a> is the second most capped player for Germany.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Birgit_Prinz" title="Birgit Prinz">Birgit Prinz</a>, a former <a href="/wiki/Captain_(association_football)" title="Captain (association football)">team captain</a> who retired after the 2011 World Cup,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> holds the record for Germany appearances, having played 214 times from 1994 to 2011. She is one of 21 German players to have reached 100 <a href="/wiki/Cap_(sport)" title="Cap (sport)">caps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caps_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-caps-86">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kerstin_Stegemann" title="Kerstin Stegemann">Kerstin Stegemann</a> is second, having played 191 times. <a href="/wiki/Bettina_Wiegmann" title="Bettina Wiegmann">Bettina Wiegmann</a>, Germany's team captain during the <a href="/wiki/2003_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2003 FIFA Women's World Cup">2003 World Cup</a> win, comes fourth with 154 games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caps_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-caps-86">[86]</a></sup> Prinz exceeded Wiegmann’s record as the most capped player in November 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Wiegmann is the only honorary captain of the German women’s national football team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>The title of Germany’s highest goalscorer is also held by Prinz. She scored her first goal in July 1994 against <a href="/wiki/Canada_women%27s_national_soccer_team" title="Canada women's national soccer team">Canada</a> and finished her career with 128 goals (averaging 0.60 goals per game).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goals_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-goals-46">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Heidi_Mohr" title="Heidi Mohr">Heidi Mohr</a>, as well as being the second-highest scorer, is also the most prolific with 83 goals coming from 104 games (averaging 0.80 goals per game).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goals_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-goals-46">[46]</a></sup> Two players share the record for goals scored in one match: <a href="/wiki/Conny_Pohlers" title="Conny Pohlers">Conny Pohlers</a> scored five goals in October 2001 against <a href="/wiki/Portugal_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Portugal women's national football team">Portugal</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Inka_Grings" title="Inka Grings">Inka Grings</a> scored five times in February 2004, again facing Portugal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Silvia_Neid" title="Silvia Neid">Silvia Neid</a>, the former German national coach, is the sixth highest goalscorer with 48 goals in 111 games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goals_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-goals-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>The largest margin of victory achieved by Germany is 17–0 against Kazakhstan during a <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Women%27s_Championship" title="UEFA Women's Championship">European Championship</a> qualifying game in November 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The record defeat, a 0–6 deficit against the <a href="/wiki/United_States_women%27s_national_soccer_team" title="United States women's national soccer team">United States</a>, occurred during a friendly match in March 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Silke_Rottenberg" title="Silke Rottenberg">Silke Rottenberg</a> has the most appearances for a goalkeeper with 126 caps and 67 games without conceding a goal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Former goalkeeper <a href="/wiki/Nadine_Angerer" title="Nadine Angerer">Nadine Angerer</a> is second, with 124 games (80 without conceding a goal).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Bettina Wiegmann holds the record of 14 goals from penalty kicks; <a href="/wiki/Renate_Lingor" title="Renate Lingor">Renate Lingor</a> comes in second with 8 goals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tina_Wunderlich" title="Tina Wunderlich">Tina Wunderlich</a> scored the team's only <a href="/wiki/Own_goal" title="Own goal">own goal</a> in the semi-final of the <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2000_Summer_Olympics" title="Football at the 2000 Summer Olympics">2000 Summer Olympics</a> against <a href="/wiki/Norway_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Norway women's national football team">Norway</a>; it was the game's only goal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>The German team also holds several international records. In 2007, they were the first to win two consecutive <a href="/wiki/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="FIFA Women's World Cup">Women's World Cup</a> titles and they achieved the biggest win in tournament history by beating <a href="/wiki/Argentina_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Argentina women's national football team">Argentina</a> 11–0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc-records_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-wc-records-31">[31]</a></sup> Germany is also the only team to win either the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">men's</a> or women's World Cup without conceding a goal and the only country to win both World Cups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc2007_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-wc2007-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> With 14 goals, Prinz became the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup#Top_scorers_of_all_time" title="FIFA Women's World Cup">overall top goalscorer</a> at the Women's World Cup in 2007,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wc-records_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-wc-records-31">[31]</a></sup> and she and Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Marta_(footballer)" title="Marta (footballer)">Marta</a> are the only women to have received the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Player_of_the_Year" title="FIFA World Player of the Year">FIFA World Player of the Year</a> award at least three times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<table>
<tr>
